{"Quotes": "Propylene serves as one of the most significant compounds in the chemical industry. Propane dehydrogenation (PDH), an on purpose propylene production technology is developing. Pt- and CrOx-based catalysts are widely applied in commercialized PDH processes, and both exhibit high activity and propylene yields. However, as an intensively endothermic process, PDH requires operation at high temperatures (generally above 500 C), which restricts the C3H6 selectivity and catalyst structure stability on account of coking side reactions, particle sintering, and so forth.", "English": "Propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) technology is developing as a promising method for propylene production, addressing the challenges of conventional production methods in terms of feedstock availability, process efficiency, and product selectivity. PDH uses Pt- and CrOx-based catalysts, which exhibit high activity and propylene yields. However, the process is endothermic and requires high temperatures, leading to issues like coking and particle sintering. Nanostructured catalysts, with tunable geometric and electronic properties, are crucial as they influence the adsorption of propyl intermediates on the catalyst surface, improving process efficiency and product selectivity.", "Chinese": "丙烷直接脱氢（PDH）技术正在发展成为一种有前景的丙烯生产方法，针对传统生产方法在原料可用性、过程效率和产品选择性方面的挑战。PDH使用Pt和CrOx基催化剂，这些催化剂表现出高活性和高丙烯产量。然而，该过程是吸热的，需要高温，导致诸如焦化和颗粒烧结等问题。具有可调几何和电子性质的纳米结构催化剂至关重要，因为这些特性影响丙基中间体在催化剂表面的吸附，从而提高过程效率和产品选择性。", "Doi": "10.1021/accountsmr.0c00012"},
{"Quotes": "", "English": "Propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) technology is advantageous for propylene production due to its ability to convert low-cost saturated hydrocarbons to value-added olefins, making it economically competitive. Unlike conventional methods which rely on steam cracking and fluidized catalytic cracking of naphtha, PDH specifically addresses the challenges of decreasing fossil oil resources and increasing market demand for propene by directly converting propane, an abundant and cheaper feedstock, into propene with high selectivity. This process is highly endothermic and equilibrium-limited, requiring high temperatures for satisfactory propene yield, but it avoids the issues of hydrocarbon cracking and coke formation that can lead to decreased catalyst activity and stability in other processes. PDH also addresses challenges in feedstock availability by utilizing propane, an important raw material in the production of various chemicals. This method improves process efficiency and product selectivity by directly converting propane to propene, bypassing the need for intermediate steps and reducing the likelihood of undesired side reactions.", "Chinese": "丙烷直接脱氢（PDH）技术在丙烯生产中具有优势，因为它能够将低成本的饱和烃转化为高价值的烯烃，使其在经济上具有竞争力。与依赖于蒸汽裂解和催化裂化石脑油的常规方法不同，PDH特别解决了化石油资源持续减少和丙烯市场需求增加的挑战，通过直接将丰富的、成本更低的丙烷转化为丙烯，具有高选择性。该过程是高度吸热和平衡受限的，需要高温才能获得满意的丙烯产量，但它避免了其他过程中可能出现的烃类裂解和焦炭形成问题，这些问题会导致催化剂活性和稳定性下降。PDH通过利用丙烷，即在各种化学品生产中重要的原材料，特别解决了原料供应的挑战。该方法通过直接将丙烷转化为丙烯，省去了中间步骤，减少了不希望的副反应的可能性，从而提高了过程效率和产品选择性。", "Doi": "10.1016/S1872-2067(17)62805-5"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Propylene is one of the most important petrochemical building blocks for the production of a diverse range of products, from solvents to plastics and other valuable intermediates, for example, polypropylene, acrylonitrile, acrolein, and acrylic acid.\"\n          [2] \"Because of an increasing demand of propylene and the depletion of petroleum resources, on-purposed propylene production technologies from alternative substrates, such as methanol-to-olefin (MTO), [3,4] metathesis of ethylene and butylene [5] and propane dehydrogenation (PDH) [6] have been intensively developed.\"\n          [3] \"Among these technologies, propane dehydrogenation is the most economical and shortest route for propylene production and is commercially available, developed by several licensing processes including the Catofin process from CB&I Lummus, reference [7] and the Oleflex process from Universal Oil Products (or Honeywell UOP) [8].\"\n          [4] \"Because this reaction is a highly endothermic process and equilibrium-limited reaction, the relatively high temperature (above 550 C) is usually required for promoting the catalytic activity.\"\n          [5] \"Thus, this behavior facilitates several side-reactions, in particular, thermal/catalytic cracking and coke formation, resulting in the low propylene selectivity and the rapid deactivation of catalysts [9].\"\n          [6] \"It is, therefore, a great challenge to develop this process, especially in terms of increasing the efficiency of catalysts exhibiting high selectivity and stability in such severe operating conditions for the industrial production of propylene.\"", "English": "Propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) technology is the most promising method for propylene production due to its economic and process efficiency advantages. It addresses the challenges of conventional production methods by providing an alternative route that is not dependent on petroleum resources, which are becoming increasingly scarce. PDH is a commercially available technology that can directly convert propane into propylene, making it the most economical and shortest route for propylene production. However, the PDH process is highly endothermic and equilibrium-limited, requiring high temperatures to promote catalytic activity. This high-temperature operation facilitates side reactions such as thermal/catalytic cracking and coke formation, leading to low propylene selectivity and rapid catalyst deactivation. Therefore, the development of PDH technology, especially in terms of improving the efficiency of catalysts with high selectivity and stability under severe operating conditions, is a significant challenge for the industrial production of propylene.", "Chinese": "丙烷直接脱氢（PDH）技术是生产丙烯最有前途的方法，因为它在经济性和工艺效率方面具有优势。它通过提供一条不依赖于日益稀缺的石油资源的替代路线，解决了传统生产方法面临的挑战。PDH是一种可商业获得的技术，可以直接将丙烷转化为丙烯，使其成为生产丙烯最经济、最短的途径。然而，PDH过程是高度吸热和平衡限制的，需要高温来促进催化活性。这种高温操作促进了热/催化裂解和焦炭形成等副反应，导致丙烯选择性低和催化剂快速失活。因此，PDH技术的发展，特别是在提高催化剂在恶劣操作条件下具有高选择性和稳定性的效率方面，是工业生产丙烯的重大挑战。", "Doi": "10.3390/catal9020174"},

Compared to oil-based cracking technologies, PDH uses natural or shale gas as feedstock, which contains significantly fewer impurities, making the process cleaner and more sustainable. The growing contribution of PDH to overall propylene production is evidenced by the construction of new plants. However, conventional PDH catalysts containing CrOx or Pt species have drawbacks. CrOx catalysts pose health risks due to the toxicity of Cr(VI), while Pt-based catalysts are expensive and prone to deactivation from Pt sintering. The use of Cl2 or Cl-containing compounds to re-disperse platinum is also environmentally harmful. Recently, eco-friendly and cost-efficient catalysts based on ZrO2 have been developed, but they require promotion with metal oxides and contain supported Ru or Rh NP to show high activity in the non-oxidative dehydrogenation of propane, n-butane, or iso-butane.", "Chinese": "丙烷直接脱氢（PDH）技术被认为是生产丙烯最有前景的方法，因为它具有环保性、过程效率和产品选择性。与基于石油的裂解技术相比，PDH使用天然气或页岩气作为原料，其中的杂质显著减少。这使得该过程更清洁、更可持续。PDH对整体丙烯生产日益增长的贡献体现在新工厂的建设上。然而，含有CrOx或Pt物种的传统PDH催化剂存在缺点。CrOx催化剂由于Cr(VI)的毒性而构成健康风险，而基于Pt的催化剂价格昂贵，且易因Pt烧结而失活。使用Cl2或含Cl化合物重新分散铂也是对环境有害的。最近，已经开发出了基于ZrO2的环保且成本高效的催化剂，但它们需要金属氧化物的促进，并且含有支持的Ru或Rh NP，以在丙烷、n-丁烷或异丁烷的非氧化脱氢中表现出高活性。", "Doi": "10.1038/s41467-018-06174-5"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"Direct dehydrogenation can be performed either with or without an oxidant present, and O2, CO2 and N2O can all participate as oxidants to form propene from propane.\"\n          [2] \"The discovery of active and selective catalysts is central to the development of these on-purpose technologies and is the focus of the current review.\"\n          [3] \"Research into propane dehydrogenation is an ever-growing field and to date there are several review articles available on direct dehydrogenation (DDH) (Sattler,2 2013), soft oxidation with CO2 (ODH-CO2) (Atanga,3 2018) and oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH-O2) (Carrero,4 2014 and Shi,5 2018).\"\n          [4] \"The scope of this article is to critically review the literature on DDH, ODH-CO2 and ODH-O2 to identify over-arching themes and common rules for catalyst design.\"\n          [5] \"We do not try to capture aspects of reaction design and engineering, which itself is a vibrant field of research.\"\n          [6] \"We attempt to compare the performance of catalysts in the different sub-reactions by calculating a space time yield (STY), (measured in molC3H6 kgcat1 h1), which is based on the initial activity of the catalysts.\"\n          [7] \"Additionally, we emphasise the environmental and industrial perspectives of each reaction and the prospects for industrial application.\"\n          [8] \"Propene is a major platform chemical with a myriad of uses in the manufacturing and chemical industry.\"\n          [9] \"The majority of propene is used to manufacture polypropene, a recyclable thermoplastic polymer which is second only to polyethene in the scale of production.\"\n          [10] \"Polypropene has favourable physical properties such as high chemical resistance and good elasticity, which makes it useful in a broad range of applications, including packaging, manufacturing and in construction materials.\"\n          [11] \"Propene is also the precursor to many other polymer-related chemicals, including propene oxide (used to make polyurethane), acrylic acid (used to make acrylic polymers) and acrylonitrile (used to make polypropenonitrile).\"\n          [12] \"Additionally, large scale chemicals such as isopropanol and cumene (used to manufacture phenol and acetone) are made from propene.\"\n          [13] \"Annual propene production was around 130 megatonnes in 20191 and is predicted to increase to 191 megatonnes by 2030.\"\n          [14] \"Significantly, the expected growth in propane demand cannot be met by existing processes, which are based on steam-cracking (SC) and fluid catalytic cracking (FCC).\"\n          [15] \"Instead, direct or on-purpose processes are being developed to fill the so-called propene gap.\"\n          [16] \"The focus of this critical review is on the dehydrogenation of propane.\"\n          [17] \"The environmental and economic aspects of different existing propene production technologies are important considerations when reviewing the feasibility of newer technologies based on dehydrogenation.\"\n          [18] \"Green chemistry principles, such as atom economy, the use of toxic chemicals and the renewability of the feedstock, are especially relevant.\"\n          [19] \"This is summarised in Table 1, which compares steam cracking, MTO (from coal and natural gas) and dehydrogenation.\"\n          [20] \"Atom economy is a measurement of the percentage of atomic mass in the starting reagents that ends up in the final product.\"\n          [21] \"It serves as a useful indicator of how chemically efficient a process could be.\"\n          [22] \"It is important to remember that in practice, the real atom economy is determined by the selectivity of the catalyst, and the prevalence of side-reactions.\"\n          [23] \"Although it is not possible to precisely calculate the atom economy of SC or MTO due to the complexity of the process and uncertainty in the composition of the feedstock, it is apparent that both of these technologies are inferior to dehydrogenation.\"\n          [24] \"In the case of SC a wide range of chemicals are made, and while these are ultimately utilised in the production of other chemicals, the % of propene formed from such a process is low.\"\n          [25] \"Equally, in the MTO process from coal or natural gas, the atom economy is limited by the generation of H2O as a by-product.\"\n          [26] \"In contrast, in the case of dehydrogenation only H2 is generated as a by-product, meaning an atom economy of 95% is possible.\"\n          [27] \"With respect to the toxicity of the chemicals, SC forms various carcinogenic compounds, and the toxicity of methanol is well-established.\"\n          [28] \"In contrast, propane is non-hazardous.\"\n          [29] \"At present each of the feedstocks used in the main methods of propene production tend to be non-renewable.\"\n          [30] \"In the case of SC and coal-based MTO, this is unavoidable due to the nature of the feedstock.\"\n          [31] \"However, natural gas and propane can be produced from biomass.\"\n          [32] \"In these cases, the feedstock is renewable and so these processes are potentially much more sustainable than when using fossil fuels.\"\n          [33] \"Rodrguez-Vallejo et al. carried out life cycle analyses of naphtha cracking, propane dehydrogenation and MTO (using coal or natural gas as feedstocks).\"\n          [34] \"In this analysis, the global warming potential (GWP), resource scarcity, impact on human health and the impact on the ecosystem was considered and quantified as a cost per kg of C3H6 produced.\"\n          [35] \"These data are shown in Table 1.\"\n          [36] \"In terms of the global warming potential of each process, the MTO based reactions are clearly worse than the naphtha cracking or direct dehydrogenation reactions, but the CO2 emissions of the coal-based feedstock is almost an order of magnitude higher than naphtha cracking.\"\n          [37] \"A similar trend was observed in the ecosystem category.\"\n          [38] \"This was measured in terms of local species lost integrated over time and underlines the gross negative impact of utilising coal as a feedstock in this chemistry.\"\n          [39] \"Resource scarcity was calculated based on the monetary burden of future mineral and fossil fuel extraction and shows that natural gas has around double the cost of that associated with propane, coal and naphtha.\"\n          [40] \"Finally, the cost to human health was estimated in terms of disability-adjusted life years, and naphtha cracking and propane dehydrogenation came out favourably compared to the MTO processes.\"\n          [41] \"Based on these metrics, it is clear that MTO from coal or natural gas has a larger environmental burden than SC or dehydrogenation.\"\n          [42] \"Opportunities for improving the sustainability of the dehydrogenation process were recently highlighted by Agarwal et al. who investigated various intensification and integration strategies.\"\n          [43] \"It was shown that such strategies, which involved additional waste heat recovery and off-gas recycling, up to 70% reductions in CO2 emissions were possible.\"\n          [44] \"The increased interest in direct propane dehydrogenation has been enhanced by the fall in propane price, which is the direct result of the discovery of vast shale gas reserves in North America.\"\n          [45] \"Although propane is a minor component in shale gas, it is facile to separate and therefore represents a valuable, but non-renewable source of propane.\"\n          [46] \"Increasing public pressure to move away from such non-renewable feedstocks continues to gain momentum and the propane production industry has responded accordingly.\"\n          [47] \"The production of renewable green propane (also called bio-propane and bioLPG) is an emerging sector which is already being operated on a large scale.\"\n          [48] \"TotalEnergies operates a bio-refinery in La Mede, which has a design capacity for bio-propane of 25000 tonnes per year.\"\n          [49] \"Additionally, Neste recently opened a renewable propane production plant, which will produce 40000 tonnes per year.\"\n          [50] \"The propane generated is then sold on to be used in direct propane dehydrogenation.\"\n          [51] \"While this is an important start, it represents less than 1% of annual propane capacity.\"\n          [52] \"Bio-propane can be produced from the processing of biomass, notably as a side-product in the production of bio-diesel16 as well as glycerol17 and other substrates.18\"\n          [53] \"With increasing pressure to source chemical feedstocks sustainably, we can expect that new interest will focus on renewable and green routes to the key platform chemicals currently required by modern society, and that green propane will play an important role in this.\"\n          [54] \"Overall, direct propane dehydrogenation satisfies many principles of green chemistry in comparison to SC and MTO benchmarks: it is highly atom-economic, does not use toxic reactants (or generate toxic by-products) and can in principle, utilise renewable feedstocks.\"\n          [55] \"However, in practice dehydrogenation will only be desirable as a means of producing propene if the reaction can operate efficiently, with minimal side-products and maximum yield.\"\n          [56] \"These factors are governed by the choice of catalyst, the main focus of the current review.\"\n          [57] \"The development of an active, selective and stable catalyst is essential for dehydrogenation to be considered as a viable process in the near- and long-term future.\"\n          [1] \"Propene is a major platform chemical with applications in the polymer and chemical industry and its place in modern society is guaranteed over the coming decades.\"\n          [2] \"At present, sources of propane are mostly non-renewable, but the development of technologies to produce renewable green propane are gaining traction, which coupled with new catalytic processes will provide the platform to produce green propene.\"\n          [3] \"Currently, non-oxidative direct dehydrogenation (DDH) is the only commercialised process, and this is reflected in the high spacetime yield commonly reported over the most active catalysts which are typically Pt or Cr based.\"\n          [4] \"However, the formation of coke over even the most selective catalysts necessitates multi-reactor cycling to facilitate regeneration.\"\n          [5] \"Oxidative dehydrogenation using O2 (ODH-O2) does not suffer from coke formation, but can lead to overoxidation, limiting the yield of propene.\"\n          [6] \"While no commercial processes have yet been developed, a promising new class of ODH-O2 catalysts has emerged in recent years which use boron as the active component.\"\n          [7] \"These catalysts are amongst the most active and selective reported to date for this reaction.\"\n          [8] \"The use of CO2 as a soft oxidant (ODH-CO2) has also gained interest due to the environmental advantages of utilising CO2.\"\n          [9] \"Although this is an attractive prospect with considerable potential, the propene yields reported over these catalysts are an order of magnitude lower than the most active DDH and ODH-O2 catalysts.\"\n          [10] \"Despite significant advances in the past decade, current ODH-CO2 catalysts remain far from displaying the activity levels necessary to be considered for commercial application.\"\n          [11] \"The specific requirements of catalyst design for each sub-reaction are discussed and we identify that, regardless of the method of dehydrogenation, the balance of acid and base sites on the catalyst surface is of paramount importance.\"\n          [12] \"Future catalyst design in DDH and ODH-O2 should focus on improving selectivity to propene, while ODH-CO2 catalysts are limited by their low intrinsic activity with respect to CO2.\"\n          [13] \"The scarcity of some common catalytic elements is also discussed, with recommendations focusing on more abundant chemical elements.\"\n          [14] \"Future research should focus on the low temperature activation of CO2 as a priority.\"\n          [15] \"With further research and development of lower energy routes to propene based on the dehydrogenation of green propane, it should be possible to transform the manufacturing landscape of this key chemical intermediate.\"", "English": "The key advantages of propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) technology that make it the most promising method for propylene production include its high spacetime yield, which is commonly reported over the most active catalysts that are typically Pt or Cr based. This technology addresses the challenges faced by conventional production methods in terms of feedstock availability, process efficiency, and product selectivity. PDH can use renewable green propane as a feedstock, which is gaining traction as a sustainable alternative to non-renewable sources. The technology also avoids the formation of coke, which necessitates multi-reactor cycling for regeneration in non-oxidative direct dehydrogenation processes. Oxidative dehydrogenation using O2 (ODH-O2) does not suffer from coke formation, but can lead to overoxidation, limiting the yield of propene. A promising new class of ODH-O2 catalysts has emerged in recent years, which use boron as the active component and are among the most active and selective reported to date for this reaction. The use of CO2 as a soft oxidant (ODH-CO2) has also gained interest due to the environmental advantages of utilizing CO2. However, the propene yields reported over these catalysts are an order of magnitude lower than the most active DDH and ODH-O2 catalysts. Future catalyst design in DDH and ODH-O2 should focus on improving selectivity to propene, while ODH-CO2 catalysts are limited by their low intrinsic activity with respect to CO2. The scarcity of some common catalytic elements is also discussed, with recommendations focusing on more abundant chemical elements. Future research should prioritize the low temperature activation of CO2. {"Quotes": "", "English": "Non-oxidative propane dehydrogenation (PDH) is an attractive reaction for both industrial and scientific reasons, allowing direct large-scale production of propene and fundamental analysis of C-H activation. {"Quotes": "", "English": "The literature does not provide specific information on the advantages of propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) technology in addressing the challenges of feedstock availability, process efficiency, and product selectivity compared to conventional production methods. However, it does mention that PDH is a highly endothermic reaction that requires high temperatures for high propane conversion, and that supported platinum-based catalysts are highly active for PDH but susceptible to deactivation by coke deposition. Frequent regenerations of deactivated catalyst by coke combustion are required in practice, which can lead to irreversible sintering of highly dispersed Pt species under high-temperature and oxidizing conditions. The literature also notes that anti-sintering Pt-based catalysts that are stable during cyclic coke combustion and do not require oxychlorination regeneration are highly desired, as coke deposition seems thermodynamically inevitable under PDH operation conditions. {"Quotes": "[1] \"In the past several decades, light alkane dehydrogenation to mono-olefins, especially propane dehydrogenation to propylene has gained widespread attention and much development in the field of research and commercial application.\"\n          [2] \"The dehydrogenation of alkane to olefin and H2 is endothermic, and the conversion for a single pass is limited by the thermodynamic equilibrium.\"\n          [3] \"Theoretically, propane dehydrogenation (PDH) is an atom-economic reaction. That is, one mole of propane generates one mole propylene and one mole H2 through dehydrogenation, and both of the products are high value-added.\"\n          [4] \"Although it is impossible to achieve the ideal state in practice, the actual results can approach this state very closely via the application of both an appropriate catalyst and the optimized reaction conditions.\"\n          [5] \"PDH is a high energy-consuming process.\"\n          [6] \"Obviously, increasing the conversion of propane for a single pass is an effective way to reduce the equipment investment and operation cost; however, it is not simple.\"\n          [7] \"To increase the conversion of propane, from the view of thermodynamic equilibrium, there are three measures we can take: increasing the reaction temperature, reducing the partial pressure of hydrocarbons and lowering the partial pressure of H2.\"\n          [8] \"Thus, the actual reaction temperature and pressure for dehydrogenation plants should be determined by the variation trend of the equilibrium conversion with temperature and pressure as well as the severity of the unselective thermal reactions.\"\n          [9] \"Suitable reaction conditions are important not only for a commercial unit, but also for the exploration of new catalysts, which means that it is not worth carrying out an in-depth study on a catalyst possessing good performance only under the reaction conditions detached from engineering practice.\"", "English": "Propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) technology has gained widespread attention and development in recent decades. PDH is an atom-economic reaction, where one mole of propane generates one mole of propylene and one mole of H2, both of which are high value-added products. The reaction is endothermic and limited by thermodynamic equilibrium, but with appropriate catalysts and optimized reaction conditions, the actual results can closely approach the ideal state. PDH is a high energy-consuming process, and increasing the single-pass conversion of propane is an effective way to reduce equipment investment and operation costs. To achieve this, measures such as increasing the reaction temperature, reducing the partial pressure of hydrocarbons, and lowering the partial pressure of H2 can be taken. The actual reaction conditions for dehydrogenation plants should be determined based on the equilibrium conversion trends with temperature and pressure, as well as the severity of unselective thermal reactions. Suitable reaction conditions are crucial for both commercial units and the development of new catalysts. In-depth studies on catalysts with good performance under conditions that are not detached from engineering practice are valuable.  [6] \"Unlike olefin molecules possessing an unsaturated CC bond, ethane and propane consisting only of CC and CH bonds are more difficult to adsorb on the catalyst surface and to be activated.\"\n          [7] \"Therefore, efficient and selective activation of the CH bond with suppression of the CC cleavage is the most important factor for determining the performances of the EDH and PDH catalysts.\"\n          [8] \"Though the geometric effect favors the activation of the CH bond rather than the CC bond for the two alkanes, the catalytic selectivity control of the CH cleavage than the CC cleavage still cannot be ignored in view of their average binding energies, which are 347 and 414 kJ mol1 for their CC and CH bonds, respectively.\"\n          [9] \"Only one CC double bond exists in ethylene with a binding energy of 720 kJ mol1, whereas the CC single bond is the weakest with a binding energy of 414 kJ mol1 for propylene.\"\n          [10] \"Furthermore, in addition to being readily desorbed as the target products, the olefins can also serve as the intermediates to be involved in further transformation reactions for coke formation or low-carbon by-products, owing to stronger adsorption of olefins relative to alkanes.\"\n          [11] \"Taking such differences in the adsorption-desorption properties into account, the utilization of appropriate catalysts is necessary to achieve high selectivity toward olefins for both DH reactions, especially for the PDH reaction, considering a lower stability of propylene relative to ethylene.\"", "English": "The key production methods in light of the growing global demand for olefins. This exclusive selectivity to olefins addresses the challenges of conventional production methods in terms of feedstock availability, process efficiency, and product selectivity. {"Quotes": "", "English": "The growing global demand for propylene, together with the increasing availability of propane from clean energy sources such as shale gas, has spurred interest in on-purpose production of propylene via direct dehydrogenation. Propane dehydrogenation (PDH) is one of the most effective methods to produce propylene. It specifically addresses the challenges faced by conventional production methods in terms of feedstock availability, process efficiency, and product selectivity by utilizing propane, which is widely available from various sources including natural gas and refinery off-gases, as the feedstock. The PDH process operates at relatively mild conditions compared to steam cracking, which is the conventional method for propylene production, and it can achieve high propylene yields with minimal byproducts. By directly converting propane to propylene, PDH offers high product selectivity, minimizing the production of unwanted byproducts. {"Quotes": "[1] \"As far as the commercial PDH processes are concerned, the famous ones include the Catofin process owned by CB&I Lummus, Oleflex process developed by UOP, and STAR process developed by Uhde, where the Cr/Al2O3, PtSn/Al2O3, and PtSn/ZnO-Al2O3 oxides catalysts are applied, respectively. Besides the catalytic system and the operation conditions especially the pressure, the other features like the type of reactor could also differ among the three processes.\"\n          [2] \"It has been proved that PtSn based catalysts could efficiently catalyze the direct dehydrogenation of propane, while the catalyst deactivation and the long-term stability issues are still the big challenges even for the aforementioned commercial catalysts.\"\n          [3] \"Many efforts have been made to improve the resistance to coke and to protect the active sites from aggregating, both of which are considered as the main reasons for catalyst deactivation.\"\n          [4] \"The recent boom in natural gas production including the exploitation of shale gas supplies abundant, economical, and steady sources of light alkanes like methane, ethane, and propane.\"\n          [5] \"The catalytic dehydrogenation of propane to propylene could be carried out in the presence or absence of oxidants.\"\n          [6] \"The propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) becomes a more favorable method to produce propylene because of (i) simpler reactant and product distribution, (ii) less investment, and (iii) easier integration with present technology.\"\n          [7] \"The catalytic dehydrogenation of propane to propylene could be carried out in the presence or absence of oxidants. The oxidative dehydrogenation of propane with molecular oxygen involves an exothermic process, meaning that the reaction could be operated at a medium high temperature (normally <600 C). At the same time, the existence of oxygen in the feedstock is helpful to restrict the coke formation, but the drawbacks are also obvious because the activation of CH bonds in alkanes is difficult, and deep oxidation usually occurs, leading to the generation of thermodynamically stable products COx. (7,8) The soft oxidant CO2 seems to be a good choice to overcome the problem, while it brings another challenge concerning the activation of CO2 to have it efficiently participate the reaction. {"Quotes": "", "English": "The key advantages of propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) technology that make it the most promising method for propylene production include its ability to address challenges faced by conventional production methods in terms of feedstock availability, process efficiency, and product selectivity. PDH technology can utilize abundant shale gas resources as feedstock, which are widely available and cost-effective compared to traditional feedstocks like naphtha. The process efficiency of PDH is high, with a high conversion rate of propane to propylene, and it offers a high selectivity for propylene production, minimizing the formation of by-products. Additionally, PDH technology can be implemented in a modular fashion, allowing for flexible production capacity and lower capital costs compared to large-scale petrochemical plants. It is highlighted as an on-purpose non-oxidative process that has caught much attention recently, driven by the development of shale gas exploration technology. It is suggested as an alternative production route for propylene with high efficiency and low cost, compared to conventional steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Direct dehydrogenation can be performed either with or without an oxidant present, and O2, CO2 and N2O can all participate as oxidants to form propene from propane.\"\n          [2] \"The discovery of active and selective catalysts is central to the development of these on-purpose technologies and is the focus of the current review.\"\n          [3] \"Research into propane dehydrogenation is an ever-growing field and to date there are several review articles available on direct dehydrogenation (DDH) (Sattler,2 2013), soft oxidation with CO2 (ODH-CO2) (Atanga,3 2018) and oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH-O2) (Carrero,4 2014 and Shi,5 2018).\"\n          [4] \"The scope of this article is to critically review the literature on DDH, ODH-CO2 and ODH-O2 to identify over-arching themes and common rules for catalyst design.\"\n          [5] \"We do not try to capture aspects of reaction design and engineering, which itself is a vibrant field of research.\"\n          [6] \"We attempt to compare the performance of catalysts in the different sub-reactions by calculating a space time yield (STY), (measured in molC3H6 kgcat1 h1), which is based on the initial activity of the catalysts.\"\n          [7] \"Additionally, we emphasise the environmental and industrial perspectives of each reaction and the prospects for industrial application.\"\n          [8] \"Propene is a major platform chemical with a myriad of uses in the manufacturing and chemical industry.\"\n          [9] \"The majority of propene is used to manufacture polypropene, a recyclable thermoplastic polymer which is second only to polyethene in the scale of production.\"\n          [10] \"Polypropene has favourable physical properties such as high chemical resistance and good elasticity, which makes it useful in a broad range of applications, including packaging, manufacturing and in construction materials.\"\n          [11] \"Propene is also the precursor to many other polymer-related chemicals, including propene oxide (used to make polyurethane), acrylic acid (used to make acrylic polymers) and acrylonitrile (used to make polypropenonitrile).\"\n          [12] \"Additionally, large scale chemicals such as isopropanol and cumene (used to manufacture phenol and acetone) are made from propene.\"\n          [13] \"Annual propene production was around 130 megatonnes in 20191 and is predicted to increase to 191 megatonnes by 2030.\"\n          [14] \"Significantly, the expected growth in propane demand cannot be met by existing processes, which are based on steam-cracking (SC) and fluid catalytic cracking (FCC).\"\n          [15] \"Instead, direct or on-purpose processes are being developed to fill the so-called propene gap.\"\n          [16] \"The focus of this critical review is on the dehydrogenation of propane.\"\n          [17] \"The environmental and economic aspects of different existing propene production technologies are important considerations when reviewing the feasibility of newer technologies based on dehydrogenation.\"\n          [18] \"Green chemistry principles, such as atom economy, the use of toxic chemicals and the renewability of the feedstock, are especially relevant.\"\n          [19] \"This is summarised in Table 1, which compares steam cracking, MTO (from coal and natural gas) and dehydrogenation.\"\n          [20] \"Atom economy is a measurement of the percentage of atomic mass in the starting reagents that ends up in the final product.\"\n          [21] \"It serves as a useful indicator of how chemically efficient a process could be.\"\n          [22] \"It is important to remember that in practice, the real atom economy is determined by the selectivity of the catalyst, and the prevalence of side-reactions.\"\n          [23] \"Although it is not possible to precisely calculate the atom economy of SC or MTO due to the complexity of the process and uncertainty in the composition of the feedstock, it is apparent that both of these technologies are inferior to dehydrogenation.\"\n          [24] \"In the case of SC a wide range of chemicals are made, and while these are ultimately utilised in the production of other chemicals, the % of propene formed from such a process is low.\"\n          [25] \"Equally, in the MTO process from coal or natural gas, the atom economy is limited by the generation of H2O as a by-product.\"\n          [26] \"In contrast, in the case of dehydrogenation only H2 is generated as a by-product, meaning an atom economy of 95% is possible.\"\n          [27] \"With respect to the toxicity of the chemicals, SC forms various carcinogenic compounds, and the toxicity of methanol is well-established.\"\n          [28] \"In contrast, propane is non-hazardous.\"\n          [29] \"At present each of the feedstocks used in the main methods of propene production tend to be non-renewable.\"\n          [30] \"In the case of SC and coal-based MTO, this is unavoidable due to the nature of the feedstock.\"\n          [31] \"However, natural gas and propane can be produced from biomass.\"\n          [32] \"In these cases, the feedstock is renewable and so these processes are potentially much more sustainable than when using fossil fuels.\"\n          [33] \"Rodrguez-Vallejo et al. carried out life cycle analyses of naphtha cracking, of magnitude higher than naphtha cracking.\"\n          [37] \"A similar trend was observed in the ecosystem category.\"\n          [38] \"This was measured in terms of local species lost integrated over time and underlines the gross negative impact of utilising coal as a feedstock in this chemistry.\"\n          [39] \"Resource scarcity was calculated based on the monetary burden of future propane will play an important role in this.\"\n          [54] \"Overall, direct propane dehydrogenation satisfies many principles of green chemistry in comparison to SC and MTO benchmarks: it is highly atom-economic, does not use toxic reactants (or generate toxic by-products) and can in principle, utilise renewable feedstocks.\"\n          [55] \"However, in practice dehydrogenation will only be desirable as a means of producing propene if the reaction can operate efficiently, with minimal side-products and maximum yield.\"\n          [56] \"These factors are governed by the choice of catalyst, the main focus of the current review.\"\n          [57] \"The development of an active, selective and stable catalyst is essential for dehydrogenation to be considered as a viable process in the near- and long-term future.\"\n          [1] \"Propene is a major platform chemical with applications in the polymer and chemical industry and its place in modern society is guaranteed over the for propylene production include its high spacetime yield, which is commonly reported over the most active catalysts that are typically Pt or Cr based. This technology addresses the challenges faced by conventional production methods in terms of feedstock availability, process efficiency, and product selectivity. PDH can use renewable green propane as a feedstock, which is gaining traction as a sustainable alternative to non-renewable sources. The technology also avoids the formation of coke, which necessitates multi-reactor cycling for regeneration in non-oxidative direct dehydrogenation processes. Oxidative dehydrogenation using O2 (ODH-O2) does not suffer from coke formation, but can lead to overoxidation, limiting the yield of propene. A promising new class of ODH-O2 catalysts has emerged in recent years, which use boron as the active component and are among the most active and selective reported to date for this reaction. The use of CO2 as a soft oxidant (ODH-CO2) has also gained interest due to the environmental advantages of utilizing CO2. However, the propene yields reported over these catalysts are an order of magnitude lower than the most active DDH and ODH-O2 catalysts. Future catalyst design in DDH and ODH-O2 should focus on improving selectivity to propene, while ODH-CO2 catalysts are limited by their low intrinsic activity with respect to CO2. The scarcity of some common catalytic elements is also discussed, with recommendations focusing on more abundant chemical elements. {"Quotes": "[1] \"The gap between supply and demand of propylene has become more and more evident, because of a large consumption of the downstream products derived from propylene. Propane dehydrogenation (PDH) constitutes an important alternative for the production of propylene, and thus considerable attention has been paid to the development of eco-friendly and cost-efficient catalysts for this process.\"\n          [2] \"Among these technologies, propane dehydrogenation attracts much attention in the current industrial context, because the cheap propane is widely available from abundant shale gas.\"\n          [3] \"Furthermore, high selectivity to propylene can be achieved with the valuable H2 as the by-product in the propane dehydrogenation process.\"", "English": "Propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) technology is the most promising method for propylene production due to its ability to address the challenges faced by conventional production methods. The growing global demand for propylene has led to a significant gap between supply and demand, as downstream products derived from propylene are consumed in large quantities. PDH technology offers an important alternative for propylene production, attracting considerable attention for the development of eco-friendly and cost-efficient catalysts. This technology is particularly advantageous in the current industrial context due to the availability of cheap propane from abundant shale gas, which can be converted into propylene with high selectivity and valuable hydrogen as a by-product. {"Quotes": "", "English": "The literature does not provide specific information on the advantages of propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) technology in addressing the challenges of feedstock availability, process efficiency, and product selectivity compared to conventional production methods. Frequent regenerations of deactivated catalyst by coke combustion are required in practice, which can lead to irreversible sintering of highly dispersed Pt species under high-temperature and oxidizing conditions. {"Quotes": "Propylene is one of the most important basic chemicals that can be processed into various types of value-added products, such as polymers (e.g., polypropylene, acrylonitrile, and polyesters), oxygenates (e.g., acetone and acetaldehyde), daily care products, and washing detergent.1 The conventional propylene production processes include fluid catalytic cracking and steam cracking of naphtha and light diesel.2 However, both these methods depend heavily on fossil fuels and emit a large amount of CO2. Besides, these methods still have relatively low propylene selectivity, which no longer meets the increasing demand for propylene. Thus, given these circumstances, a highly efficient way to produce propylene is urgently need, both scientifically and economically.3 In the past few years, multiple technologies for the production of propylene have been developed, such as the methanol-to-olefin (MTO) process,4,5 FischerTropsch (FT) process,6 and propane dehydrogenation (PDH).7 Particularly, PDH technology is recognized to be one of the most promising methods for the production of propylene because it is an on-purpose process to produce only propylene instead of hybrid hydrocarbon products.715 In addition, with the advent of fracking technology to produce shale gas, which contains a large amount of propane, the feedstocks for PDH have become extremely cheap. Moreover, PDH factories are highly adjustable and can be adapted to run many other different reactions. {"Quotes": "[1] \"In the past several decades, light alkane dehydrogenation to mono-olefins, especially propane dehydrogenation to propylene has gained widespread attention and much development in the field of research and commercial application.\"\n          [2] \"The dehydrogenation of alkane to olefin and H2 is endothermic, and the conversion for a single pass is limited by the thermodynamic equilibrium.\"\n          [3] \"Theoretically, propane dehydrogenation (PDH) is an atom-economic reaction. That is, one mole of propane generates one mole propylene and one mole H2 through dehydrogenation, and both of the products are high value-added.\"\n          [4] \"Although it is impossible to achieve the ideal state in practice, the actual results can approach this state very closely via the application of both an appropriate catalyst and the optimized reaction conditions.\"\n          [5] \"PDH is a high energy-consuming process.\"\n          [6] \"Obviously, increasing the conversion of propane for a single pass is an effective way to reduce the equipment investment and operation cost; however, it is not simple.\"\n          [7] \"To increase the conversion of propane, from the view of thermodynamic equilibrium, there are three measures we can take: increasing the reaction temperature, reducing the partial pressure of hydrocarbons and lowering the partial pressure of H2.\"\n          [8] \"Thus, the actual reaction temperature and pressure for dehydrogenation plants should be determined by the variation trend of the equilibrium conversion with temperature and pressure as well as the severity of the unselective thermal reactions.\"\n          [9] \"Suitable reaction conditions are important not only for a commercial unit, but also for the exploration of new catalysts, which means that it is not worth carrying out an in-depth study on a catalyst possessing good performance only under the reaction conditions detached from engineering practice.\"", "English": "Propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) technology has gained widespread attention and development in recent decades. PDH is an atom-economic reaction, where one mole of propane generates one mole of propylene and one mole of H2, both of which are high value-added products. The reaction is endothermic and limited by thermodynamic equilibrium, but with appropriate catalysts and optimized reaction conditions, the actual results can closely approach the ideal state. PDH is a high energy-consuming process, and increasing the single-pass conversion of propane is an effective way to reduce equipment investment and operation costs. To achieve this, measures such as increasing the reaction temperature, reducing the partial pressure of hydrocarbons, and lowering the partial pressure of H2 can be taken. The actual reaction conditions for dehydrogenation plants should be determined based on the equilibrium conversion trends with temperature and pressure, as well as the severity of unselective thermal reactions. Suitable reaction conditions are crucial for both commercial units and the development of new catalysts. PDH technology specifically targets the conversion of propane to propylene, which can lead to higher yields of the desired product and potentially lower operating costs.", "Chinese": "然而,PDH在工业界被认为是一种有前景的丙烯生产方法,因为它能够直接将丙烷转化为丙烯,而无需进行蒸汽裂解或流化催化裂化,这两种传统方法在原料供应、过程效率和产品选择性方面存在局限性。PDH可以使用丙烷作为原料,丙烷是天然气加工和原油精炼的副产品,与传统方法中使用的石脑油和重油相比,丙烷的供应更加丰富和稳定。PDH还提供了比蒸汽裂解和流化催化裂化更高的丙烯选择性和更低的能耗。此外,PDH可以在一个反应器中进行,这简化了过程,降低了资本和运营成本。PDH提供了更高的过程效率和产品选择性,使其成为丙烯生产中更可持续和成本效益更高的选择。PDH提供了更高的产品选择性和过程效率,使其成为传统方法的可行替代方案,特别是在丙烷供应丰富的地区。PDH提供了更高的产品选择性和过程效率,使其成为满足全球丙烯需求增长的更可持续和更具成本效益的选择。PDH工艺设计上比传统的蒸汽裂解和流化催化裂解方法更节能,产品选择性更高,后者依赖于更重的烃类原料,产生更广泛的产品。PDH技术特别针对丙烷到丙烯的转化,这可能导致所需产品产量更高,潜在运营成本更低。", "Doi": "10.1016/j.jcat.2017.02.016"},

{"Quotes": "", "English": "Propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) technology is advantageous for propylene production due to its ability to convert low-cost saturated hydrocarbons to value-added olefins, making it economically competitive. Unlike conventional methods which rely on steam cracking and fluidized catalytic cracking of naphtha, PDH specifically addresses the challenges of decreasing fossil oil resources and increasing market demand for propene by directly converting propane, an abundant and cheaper feedstock, into propene with high selectivity. This process is highly endothermic and equilibrium-limited, requiring high temperatures for satisfactory propene yield, but it avoids the issues of hydrocarbon cracking and coke formation that can lead to decreased catalyst activity and stability in other processes. PDH also addresses challenges in feedstock availability by utilizing propane, an important raw material in the production of various chemicals. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Propylene is one of the most important petrochemical building blocks for the production of a diverse range of products, from solvents to plastics and other valuable intermediates, for example, polypropylene, acrylonitrile, acrolein, and acrylic acid.\"\n          [2] \"Because of an increasing demand of propylene and the depletion of petroleum resources, on-purposed propylene production technologies from alternative substrates, such as methanol-to-olefin (MTO), [3,4] metathesis of ethylene and butylene [5] and propane dehydrogenation (PDH) [6] have been intensively developed.\"\n          [3] \"Among these technologies, propane dehydrogenation is the most economical and shortest route for propylene production and is commercially available, developed by several licensing processes including the Catofin process from CB&I Lummus, reference [7] and the Oleflex process from Universal Oil Products (or Honeywell UOP) [8].\"\n          [4] \"Because this reaction is a highly endothermic process and equilibrium-limited reaction, the relatively high temperature (above 550 C) is usually required for promoting the catalytic activity.\"\n          [5] \"Thus, this behavior facilitates several side-reactions, in particular, thermal/catalytic cracking and coke formation, resulting in the low propylene selectivity and the rapid deactivation of catalysts [9].\"\n          [6] \"It is, therefore, a great challenge to develop this process, especially in terms of increasing the efficiency of catalysts exhibiting high selectivity and stability in such severe operating conditions for the industrial production of propylene.\"", "English": "Propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) technology is the most promising method for propylene production due to its economic and process efficiency advantages. It addresses the challenges of conventional production methods by providing an alternative route that is not dependent on petroleum resources, which are becoming increasingly scarce. PDH is a commercially available technology that can directly convert propane into propylene, making it the most economical and shortest route for propylene production. However, the PDH process is highly endothermic and equilibrium-limited, requiring high temperatures to promote catalytic activity. This high-temperature operation facilitates side reactions such as thermal/catalytic cracking and coke formation, leading to low propylene selectivity and rapid catalyst deactivation. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Direct dehydrogenation can be performed either with or without an oxidant present, and O2, CO2 and N2O can all participate as oxidants to form propene from propane.\"\n          [2] \"The discovery of active and selective catalysts is central to the development of these on-purpose technologies and is the focus of the current review.\"\n          [3] \"Research into propane dehydrogenation is an ever-growing field and to date there are several review articles available on direct dehydrogenation (DDH) (Sattler,2 2013), soft oxidation with CO2 (ODH-CO2) (Atanga,3 2018) and oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH-O2) (Carrero,4 2014 and Shi,5 2018).\"\n          [4] \"The scope of this article is to critically review the literature on DDH, ODH-CO2 and ODH-O2 to identify over-arching themes and common rules for catalyst design.\"\n          [5] \"We do not try to capture aspects of reaction design and engineering, which itself is a vibrant field of research.\"\n          [6] \"We attempt to compare the performance of catalysts in the different sub-reactions by calculating a space time yield (STY), (measured in molC3H6 kgcat1 h1), which is based on the initial activity of the catalysts.\"\n          [7] \"Additionally, we emphasise the environmental and industrial perspectives of each reaction and the prospects for industrial application.\"\n          [8] \"Propene is a major platform chemical with a myriad of uses in the manufacturing and chemical industry.\"\n          [9] \"The majority of propene is used to manufacture polypropene, a recyclable thermoplastic polymer which is second only to polyethene in the scale of production.\"\n          [10] \"Polypropene has favourable physical properties such as high chemical resistance and good elasticity, which makes it useful in a broad range of applications, including packaging, manufacturing and in construction materials.\"\n          [11] \"Propene is also the precursor to many other polymer-related chemicals, including propene oxide (used to make polyurethane), acrylic acid (used to make acrylic polymers) and acrylonitrile (used to make polypropenonitrile).\"\n          [12] \"Additionally, large scale chemicals such as isopropanol and cumene (used to manufacture phenol and acetone) are made from propene.\"\n          [13] \"Annual propene production was around 130 megatonnes in 20191 and is predicted to increase to 191 megatonnes by 2030.\"\n          [14] \"Significantly, the expected growth in propane demand cannot be met by existing processes, which are based on steam-cracking (SC) and fluid catalytic cracking (FCC).\"\n          [15] \"Instead, direct or on-purpose processes are being developed to fill the so-called propene gap.\"\n          [16] \"The focus of this critical review is on the dehydrogenation of propane.\"\n          [17] \"The environmental and economic aspects of different existing propene production technologies are important considerations when reviewing the feasibility of newer technologies based on dehydrogenation.\"\n          [18] \"Green chemistry principles, such as atom economy, the use of toxic chemicals and the renewability of the feedstock, are especially relevant.\"\n          [19] \"This is summarised in Table 1, which compares steam cracking, MTO (from coal and natural gas) and dehydrogenation.\"\n          [20] \"Atom economy is a measurement of the percentage of atomic mass in the starting reagents that ends up in the final product.\"\n          [21] \"It serves as a useful indicator of how chemically efficient a process could be.\"\n          [22] \"It is important to remember that in practice, the real atom economy is determined by the selectivity of the catalyst, and the prevalence of side-reactions.\"\n          [23] \"Although it is not possible to precisely calculate the atom economy of SC or MTO due to the complexity of the process and uncertainty in the composition of the feedstock, it is apparent that both of these technologies are inferior to dehydrogenation.\"\n          [24] \"In the case of SC a wide range of chemicals are made, and while these are ultimately utilised in the production of other chemicals, the % of propene formed from such a process is low.\"\n          [25] \"Equally, in the MTO process from coal or natural gas, the atom economy is limited by the generation of H2O as a by-product.\"\n          [26] \"In contrast, in the case of dehydrogenation only H2 is generated as a by-product, meaning an atom economy of 95% is possible.\"\n          [27] \"With respect to the toxicity of the chemicals, SC forms various carcinogenic compounds, and the toxicity of methanol is well-established.\"\n          [28] \"In contrast, propane is non-hazardous.\"\n          [29] \"At present each of the feedstocks used in the main methods of propene production tend to be non-renewable.\"\n          [30] \"In the case of SC and coal-based MTO, this is unavoidable due to the nature of the feedstock.\"\n          [31] \"However, natural gas and propane can be produced from biomass.\"\n          [32] \"In these cases, the feedstock is renewable and so these processes are potentially much more sustainable than when using fossil fuels.\"\n          [33] \"Rodrguez-Vallejo et al. carried out life cycle analyses of naphtha cracking, propane dehydrogenation and MTO (using coal or natural gas as feedstocks).\"\n          [34] \"In this analysis, the global warming potential (GWP), resource scarcity, impact on human health and the impact on the ecosystem was considered and quantified as a cost per kg of C3H6 produced.\"\n          [35] \"These data are shown in Table 1.\"\n          [36] \"In terms of the global warming potential of each process, the MTO based reactions are clearly worse than the naphtha cracking or direct dehydrogenation reactions, but the CO2 emissions of the coal-based feedstock is almost an order of magnitude higher than naphtha cracking.\"\n          [37] \"A similar trend was observed in the ecosystem category.\"\n          [38] \"This was measured in terms of local species lost integrated over time and underlines the gross negative impact of utilising coal as a feedstock in this chemistry.\"\n          [39] \"Resource scarcity was calculated based on the monetary burden of future mineral and fossil fuel extraction and shows that natural gas has around double the cost of that associated with propane, coal and naphtha.\"\n          [40] \"Finally, the cost to human health was estimated in terms of disability-adjusted life years, and naphtha cracking and propane dehydrogenation came out favourably compared to the MTO processes.\"\n          [41] \"Based on these metrics, it is clear that MTO from coal or natural gas has a larger environmental burden than SC or dehydrogenation.\"\n          [42] \"Opportunities for improving the sustainability of the dehydrogenation process were recently highlighted by Agarwal et al. who investigated various intensification and integration strategies.\"\n          [43] \"It was shown that such strategies, which involved additional waste heat recovery and off-gas recycling, up to 70% reductions in CO2 emissions were possible.\"\n          [44] \"The increased interest in direct propane dehydrogenation has been enhanced by the fall in propane price, which is the direct result of the discovery of vast shale gas reserves in North America.\"\n          [45] \"Although propane is a minor component in shale gas, it is facile to separate and therefore represents a valuable, but non-renewable source of propane.\"\n          [46] \"Increasing public pressure to move away from such non-renewable feedstocks continues to gain momentum and the propane production industry has responded accordingly.\"\n          [47] \"The production of renewable green propane (also called bio-propane and bioLPG) is an emerging sector which is already being operated on a large scale.\"\n          [48] \"TotalEnergies operates a bio-refinery in La Mede, which has a design capacity for bio-propane of 25000 tonnes per year.\"\n          [49] \"Additionally, Neste recently opened a renewable propane production plant, which will produce 40000 tonnes per year.\"\n          [50] \"The propane generated is then sold on to be used in direct propane dehydrogenation.\"\n          [51] \"While this is an important start, it represents less than 1% of annual propane capacity.\"\n          [52] \"Bio-propane can be produced from the processing of biomass, notably as a side-product in the production of bio-diesel16 as well as glycerol17 and other substrates.18\"\n          [53] \"With increasing pressure to source chemical feedstocks sustainably, we can expect that new interest will focus on renewable and green routes to the key platform chemicals currently required by modern society, and that green propane will play an important role in this.\"\n          [54] \"Overall, direct propane dehydrogenation satisfies many principles of green chemistry in comparison to SC and MTO benchmarks: it is highly atom-economic, does not use toxic reactants (or generate toxic by-products) and can in principle, utilise renewable feedstocks.\"\n          [55] \"However, in practice dehydrogenation will only be desirable as a means of producing propene if the reaction can operate efficiently, with minimal side-products and maximum yield.\"\n          [56] \"These factors are governed by the choice of catalyst, the main focus of the current review.\"\n          [57] \"The development of an active, selective and stable catalyst is essential for dehydrogenation to be considered as a viable process in the near- and long-term future.\"\n          [1] \"Propene is a major platform chemical with applications in the polymer and chemical industry and its place in modern society is guaranteed over the coming decades.\"\n          [2] \"At present, sources of propane are mostly non-renewable, but the development of technologies to produce renewable green propane are gaining traction, which coupled with new catalytic processes will provide the platform to produce green propene.\"\n          [3] \"Currently, non-oxidative direct dehydrogenation (DDH) is the only commercialised process, and this is reflected in the high spacetime yield commonly reported over the most active catalysts which are typically Pt or Cr based.\"\n          [4] \"However, the formation of coke over even the most selective catalysts necessitates multi-reactor cycling to facilitate regeneration.\"\n          [5] \"Oxidative dehydrogenation using O2 (ODH-O2) does not suffer from coke formation, but can lead to overoxidation, limiting the yield of propene.\"\n          [6] \"While no commercial processes have yet been developed, a promising new class of ODH-O2 catalysts has emerged in recent years which use boron as the active component.\"\n          [7] \"These catalysts are amongst the most active and selective reported to date for this reaction.\"\n          [8] \"The use of CO2 as a soft oxidant (ODH-CO2) has also gained interest due to the environmental advantages of utilising CO2.\"\n          [9] \"Although this is an attractive prospect with considerable potential, the propene yields reported over these catalysts are an order of magnitude lower than the most active DDH and ODH-O2 catalysts.\"\n          [10] \"Despite significant advances in the past decade, current ODH-CO2 catalysts remain far from displaying the activity levels necessary to be considered for commercial application.\"\n          [11] \"The specific requirements of catalyst design for each sub-reaction are discussed and we identify that, regardless of the method of dehydrogenation, the balance of acid and base sites on the catalyst surface is of paramount importance.\"\n          [12] \"Future activation of CO2 as a priority.\"\n          [15] \"With further research and development of lower energy routes to propene based on the dehydrogenation of green propane, it should be possible to for propylene production include its high spacetime yield, which is commonly reported over the most active catalysts that are typically Pt or Cr based. This technology addresses the challenges faced by conventional production methods in terms of feedstock availability, process efficiency, and product selectivity. PDH can use renewable green propane as a feedstock, which is gaining traction as a sustainable alternative to non-renewable sources. The technology also avoids the formation of coke, which necessitates multi-reactor cycling for regeneration in non-oxidative direct dehydrogenation processes. Oxidative dehydrogenation using O2 (ODH-O2) does not suffer from coke formation, but can lead to overoxidation, limiting the yield of propene. A promising new class of ODH-O2 catalysts has emerged in recent years, which use boron as the active component and are among the most active and selective reported to date for this reaction. The use of CO2 as a soft oxidant (ODH-CO2) has also gained interest due to the environmental advantages of utilizing CO2. However, the propene yields reported over these catalysts are an order of magnitude lower than the most active DDH and ODH-O2 catalysts. This results in a more efficient and cost-effective process for propylene production. PDH technology specifically uses propane, a widely available feedstock, as the raw material, which ensures a stable and reliable source of propylene production. Moreover, PDH processes are more energy-efficient and have higher propylene yields compared to traditional methods, such as steam cracking, which often require more complex separation processes and result in lower yields of propylene. The high selectivity of PDH processes towards propylene also minimizes the production of by-products, leading to a more streamlined and cost-effective production route. PDH technology specifically uses propane, which is abundant and widely available, as the feedstock, making it less dependent on traditional oil-based feedstocks. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Propane dehydrogenation (PDH) is a commercial propylene production technology, but high reaction temperatures result in cracking and coking.\"\n          [2] \"A change of feedstock from naphtha to light alkanes, due to the emergence of shale gas and the increasing availability of natural gas, has led to an increasing development of on-purpose propane dehydrogenation (PDH) technologies.\"\n          [3] \"PDH is an extremely energy-intensive process largely due to the high positive enthalpy of the reactions (ΔrH = 124 kJ mol1), necessitating high reaction temperatures,4 ,5 ,6 which presents grand challenges for alkene selectivity and catalyst stability.\"\n          [4] \"Especially, it has been shown that larger ensembles of active sites catalyze structure-sensitive side reactions, including cracking and deep dehydrogenation, leading to the production of C1 and C2 molecules and coke.\"\n          [5] \"To tackle these challenges, the active site isolation strategy has been proved to be effective.\"", "English": "Propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) technology is becoming increasingly promising for propylene production due to the shift in feedstock from naphtha to light alkanes, driven by the emergence of shale gas and the growing availability of natural gas. This technology addresses the challenges faced by conventional production methods in terms of feedstock availability, process efficiency, and product selectivity. PDH is an energy-intensive process due to the high positive enthalpy of the reactions, requiring high reaction temperatures. This presents significant challenges for alkene selectivity and catalyst stability, as larger ensembles of active sites can catalyze structure-sensitive side reactions, such as cracking and deep dehydrogenation, leading to the production of C1 and C2 molecules and coke. To overcome these challenges, the active site isolation strategy has proven to be effective.", "Chinese": "由于页岩气的出现和天然气供应的增加，丙烷直接脱氢（PDH）技术正成为丙烯生产最有前景的方法之一，因为原料从石脑油转向了轻质烷烃。这项技术解决了传统生产方法在原料供应、过程效率和产品选择性方面面临的挑战。PDH是一种能源密集型过程，由于反应的高正焓（ΔrH = 124 kJ mol1），需要高温反应，这对烯烃的选择性和催化剂的稳定性提出了重大挑战。特别是，已经证明，较大的活性位点集合会催化结构敏感的副反应，包括裂解和深度脱氢，导致C1和C2分子以及焦炭的产生。为了克服这些挑战，活性位点隔离策略已被证明是有效的。", "Doi": "10.1016/j.chempr.2020.10.008"},
{"Quotes": "", "English": "Propane dehydrogenation (PDH) technology has gained increasing attention due to the worldwide growing demand for propene and the increasing availability of propane from shale gas. PDH is recognized as one of the most effective technologies for propene production, which is a crucial industrial raw material for the manufacture of polypropylene, acrylonitrile, propylene oxide, acetone, and other industrial products. Compared to industrial Pt and Cr-based catalysts, non-noble zinc-based catalysts offer the advantages of lower cost and nontoxicity. However, these zinc-based catalysts often suffer from limited catalytic activity and poor stability. The introduction of moderate Ni into ZnO supported Silicalite-1 zeolite can enhance both catalytic activity and stability simultaneously. PDH technology specifically addresses the challenges faced by conventional production methods in terms of feedstock availability, process efficiency, and product selectivity by utilizing widely available propane from shale gas as feedstock, achieving high conversion rates and selectivity to propene under mild conditions.", "Chinese": "由于全球对丙烯需求的不断增长和页岩气中丙烷供应的增加，丙烷直接脱氢（PDH）技术受到了越来越多的关注。PDH是生产丙烯最有效的技术之一，丙烯是生产聚丙烯、丙烯腈、环氧丙烷、丙酮等工业产品的重要工业原料。与工业Pt和Cr基催化剂相比，非贵金属锌基催化剂具有低成本和无毒性的优点。然而，它们通常存在催化活性有限和稳定性差的问题。将适量的Ni引入到ZnO支撑的Silicalite-1沸石中，可以同时提高催化活性和稳定性。PDH技术通过利用页岩气中广泛可用的丙烷作为原料，在温和条件下实现高转化率和高丙烯选择性，具体解决了传统生产方法在原料供应、过程效率和产品选择性方面的挑战。", "Doi": "10.1016/j.cjche.2022.02.015"},
{"Quotes": "Propylene is one of the most important basic chemicals that can be processed into various types of value-added products, such as polymers (e.g., polypropylene, acrylonitrile, and polyesters), oxygenates (e.g., acetone and acetaldehyde), daily care products, and washing detergent.1 The conventional propylene production processes include fluid catalytic cracking and steam cracking of naphtha and light diesel.2 However, both these methods depend heavily on fossil fuels and emit a large amount of CO2. Besides, these methods still have relatively low propylene selectivity, which no longer meets the increasing demand for propylene. Thus, given these circumstances, a highly efficient way to produce propylene is urgently need, both scientifically and economically.3 In the past few years, multiple technologies for the production of propylene have been developed, such as the methanol-to-olefin (MTO) process,4,5 FischerTropsch (FT) process,6 and propane dehydrogenation (PDH).7 Particularly, PDH technology is recognized to be one of the most promising methods for the production of propylene because it is an on-purpose process to produce only propylene instead of hybrid hydrocarbon products.715 In addition, with the advent of fracking technology to produce shale gas, which contains a large amount of propane, the feedstocks for PDH have become extremely cheap. Moreover, PDH factories are highly adjustable and can be adapted to run many other different reactions. The differences between the price of propylene and propane and the growing propylene market make PDH extraordinarily profitable.", "English": "Propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) technology is recognized as one of the most promising methods for propylene production due to its on-purpose process that specifically targets propylene production, avoiding the production of hybrid hydrocarbon products. The advent of fracking technology has made the feedstocks for PDH, which is propane-rich shale gas, extremely cheap. Moreover, PDH factories are highly adjustable and adaptable to run various reactions, making the technology highly profitable given the price difference between propylene and propane and the growing demand for propylene in the market.", "Chinese": "丙烷直接脱氢（PDH）技术因其专门针对丙烯生产，避免了混合烃类产品的产生，而被公认为是最有前景的丙烯生产方法之一。这种技术特别有助于解决传统生产方法面临的挑战，这些方法严重依赖化石燃料，排放大量二氧化碳，且丙烯选择性相对较低。随着水力压裂技术的出现，富含丙烷的页岩气成为PDH的原料，价格极低。此外，PDH工厂高度可调，能适应运行各种反应，考虑到丙烯和丙烷的价格差异以及丙烯市场日益增长的需求，这项技术的盈利能力非常突出。", "Doi": "10.1039/D2QI00653G"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"In the past several decades, light alkane dehydrogenation to mono-olefins, especially propane dehydrogenation to propylene has gained widespread attention and much development in the field of research and commercial application.\"\n          [2] \"The dehydrogenation of alkane to olefin and H2 is endothermic, and the conversion for a single pass is limited by the thermodynamic equilibrium.\"\n          [3] \"Theoretically, propane dehydrogenation (PDH) is an atom-economic reaction. That is, one mole of propane generates one mole propylene and one mole H2 through dehydrogenation, and both of the products are high value-added.\"\n          [4] \"Although it is impossible to achieve the ideal state in practice, the actual results can approach this state very closely via the application of both an appropriate catalyst and the optimized reaction conditions.\"\n          [5] \"PDH is a high energy-consuming process.\"\n          [6] \"Obviously, increasing the conversion of propane for a single pass is an effective way to reduce the equipment investment and operation cost; however, it is not simple.\"\n          [7] \"To increase the conversion of propane, from the view of thermodynamic equilibrium, there are three measures we can take: increasing the reaction temperature, reducing the partial pressure of hydrocarbons and lowering the partial pressure of H2.\"\n          [8] \"Thus, the actual reaction temperature and pressure for dehydrogenation plants should be determined by the variation trend of the equilibrium conversion with temperature and pressure as well as the severity of the unselective thermal reactions.\"\n          [9] \"Suitable reaction conditions are important not only for a commercial unit, but also for the exploration of new catalysts, which means that it is not worth carrying out an in-depth study on a catalyst possessing good performance only under the reaction conditions detached from engineering practice.\"", "English": "Propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) technology has gained widespread attention and development in recent decades. PDH is an atom-economic reaction, where one mole of propane generates one mole of propylene and one mole of H2, both of which are high value-added products. The reaction is endothermic and limited by thermodynamic equilibrium, but with appropriate catalysts and optimized reaction conditions, the actual results can closely approach the ideal state. PDH is a high energy-consuming process, and increasing the single-pass conversion of propane is an effective way to reduce equipment investment and operation costs. To achieve this, measures such as increasing the reaction temperature, reducing the partial pressure of hydrocarbons, and lowering the partial pressure of H2 can be taken. The actual reaction conditions for dehydrogenation plants should be determined based on the equilibrium conversion trends with temperature and pressure, as well as the severity of unselective thermal reactions. Suitable reaction conditions are crucial for both commercial units and the development of new catalysts. In-depth studies on catalysts with good performance under conditions that are not detached from engineering practice are valuable. PDH technology specifically addresses the challenges faced by conventional production methods in terms of feedstock availability, process efficiency, and product selectivity by using propane as a feedstock, which is widely available and has a high conversion rate to propylene. The process is highly efficient and selective, with high propylene yield and low by-product formation.", "Chinese": "丙烷直接脱氢(PDH)技术在近几十年来受到了广泛关注和发展。PDH是一种原子经济反应，一摩尔丙烷通过脱氢生成一摩尔丙烯和一摩尔H2，这两种产品都是高附加值的。该反应是吸热的，受热力学平衡的限制，但通过适当的催化剂和优化的反应条件，实际结果可以非常接近理想状态。PDH是一个高能耗的过程，提高丙烷的单程转化率是减少设备投资和运营成本的有效途径。为了实现这一点，可以采取增加反应温度、降低碳氢化合物的分压和降低H2分压等措施。脱氢工厂的实际反应条件应根据温度和压力下平衡转化率的趋势以及非选择性热反应的严重程度来确定。适合的反应条件对于商业装置和新型催化剂的开发都至关重要。对在工程实践中不脱离条件具有良好性能的催化剂进行深入研究是很有价值的。PDH技术通过使用丙烷作为原料，解决了传统生产方法在原料可用性、过程效率和产品选择性方面面临的挑战。丙烷广泛可用，丙烯转化率高。该工艺效率高，选择性好，丙烯产量高，副产品形成少。", "Doi": "10.1039/D0CS00983K"},

{"Quotes": "", "English": "The growing global demand for propylene, together with the increasing availability of propane from clean energy sources such as shale gas, has spurred interest in on-purpose production of propylene via direct dehydrogenation. Propane dehydrogenation (PDH) is one of the most effective methods to produce propylene. It specifically addresses the challenges faced by conventional production methods in terms of feedstock availability, process efficiency, and product selectivity by utilizing propane, which is widely available from various sources including natural gas and refinery off-gases, as the feedstock. The PDH process operates at relatively mild conditions compared to steam cracking, which is the conventional method for propylene production, and it can achieve high propylene yields with minimal byproducts. This makes PDH a more efficient and selective process for propylene production. Additionally, the use of Pt-based catalysts in PDH processes provides superior activation of paraffinic CH bonds and low activity to CC cleavage, leading to high propylene selectivity. By directly converting propane to propylene, PDH offers high product selectivity, minimizing the production of unwanted byproducts. It also improves process efficiency by operating at lower temperatures and pressures than steam cracking, resulting in lower energy consumption and potentially lower greenhouse gas emissions. {"Quotes": "[1] \"As far as the commercial PDH processes are concerned, the famous ones include the Catofin process owned by CB&I Lummus, Oleflex process developed by UOP, and STAR process developed by Uhde, where the Cr/Al2O3, PtSn/Al2O3, and PtSn/ZnO-Al2O3 oxides catalysts are applied, respectively. The Catofin process is operated at slightly positive atmospheric pressure of 0.20.5 bar, and the Oleflex process is usually operated at 13 bar, while the STAR process needs a higher pressure of 56 bar. Besides the catalytic system and the operation conditions especially the pressure, the other features like the type of reactor could also differ among the three processes.\"\n          [2] \"It has been proved that PtSn based catalysts could efficiently catalyze the direct dehydrogenation of propane, while the catalyst deactivation and the long-term stability issues are still the big challenges even for the aforementioned commercial catalysts.\"\n          [3] \"Many efforts have been made to improve the resistance to coke and to protect the active sites from aggregating, both of which are considered as the main reasons for catalyst deactivation.\"\n          [4] \"The recent boom in natural gas production including the exploitation of shale gas supplies abundant, economical, and steady sources of light alkanes like methane, ethane, and propane.\"\n          [5] \"The catalytic dehydrogenation of propane to propylene could be carried out in the presence or absence of oxidants.\"\n          [6] \"The propane direct dehydrogenation (PDH) becomes a more favorable method to produce propylene because of (i) simpler reactant and product distribution, (ii) less investment, and (iii) easier integration with present technology.\"\n          [7] \"The catalytic dehydrogenation of propane to propylene could be carried out in the presence or absence of oxidants. The oxidative dehydrogenation of propane with molecular oxygen involves an exothermic process, meaning that the reaction could be operated at a medium high temperature (normally <600 C). At the same time, the existence of oxygen in the feedstock is helpful to restrict the coke formation, but the drawbacks are also obvious because the activation of CH bonds in alkanes is difficult, and deep oxidation usually occurs, leading to the generation of thermodynamically stable products COx. (7,8) The soft oxidant CO2 seems to be a good choice to overcome the problem, while it brings another challenge concerning the activation of CO2 to have it efficiently participate the reaction. This is in contrast to conventional processes such as steam cracking and fluid catalytic cracking (FCC) of naphtha, which are more complex and require higher investment.
